🤔 AI effective tips collection 🧠
❓:Given a hyperbola C centered at the origin with its right focus at (2,0) and a real axis length of 2 sqrt{3}.(1) Find the equation of the hyperbola C.(2) If the line l: y = kx + sqrt{2} intersects the left branch of the hyperbola C at points A and B, find the range of k.

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to find the equation of a hyperbola centered at the origin. The problem states that the hyperbola has its right focus at (2, 0) and a real axis length of 2√3. Then, part two involves finding the range of k for a line that intersects the left branch of the hyperbola at two points. Let me start with part one first.First, recalling the standard form of a hyperbola. Since the foci are on the x-axis (since one focus is at (2,0) and it's centered at the origin), the standard form should be (x²/a²) - (y²/b²) = 1. Here, 'a' is the distance from the center to each vertex along the x-axis, and 'c' is the distance from the center to each focus. The relationship between a, b, and c for hyperbolas is c² = a² + b².The real axis length is the length of the transverse axis, which is 2a. So, if the real axis length is 2√3, then 2a = 2√3, so a = √3. Got that.We also know that the right focus is at (2,0), so c = 2. Since c is the distance from the center to the focus, and the hyperbola is centered at the origin, c = 2. Now, using the relationship c² = a² + b², we can solve for b².Given that a = √3, a² = (√3)² = 3. c² = 2² = 4. So, 4 = 3 + b². Therefore, b² = 4 - 3 = 1. So, b = 1.Therefore, plugging back into the standard equation: (x²)/3 - (y²)/1 = 1. Simplifying, that's x²/3 - y² = 1. So that's the equation of hyperbola C. Let me double-check. The center is at (0,0), foci at (±2,0), vertices at (±√3, 0). That makes sense since the distance from center to vertex is a = √3, and distance from center to focus is c = 2. And since c > a for hyperbolas, which it is here (2 > √3 ≈1.732), that checks out. So part (1) seems done. The equation is x²/3 - y² = 1.Moving on to part (2): The line l: y = kx + √2 intersects the left branch of the hyperbola C at points A and B. Find the range of k.First, the hyperbola has two branches. The left branch corresponds to x ≤ -a, which is x ≤ -√3. The right branch is x ≥ √3. Since the line intersects the left branch at two points, the system of equations (the hyperbola and the line) should have two real solutions, both with x ≤ -√3.So, we need to substitute y = kx + √2 into the hyperbola equation and find for which k there are two real solutions, and then ensure that both solutions are on the left branch.Let me substitute y into the hyperbola equation:x²/3 - (kx + √2)² = 1.Expanding the (kx + √2)² term: (k²x² + 2kx√2 + 2).So, substituting back into the equation:x²/3 - [k²x² + 2√2 kx + 2] = 1.Let me distribute the negative sign:x²/3 - k²x² - 2√2 kx - 2 = 1.Combine like terms:x²(1/3 - k²) - 2√2 kx - 3 = 0.Multiply through by 3 to eliminate the denominator (to make it easier):x²(1 - 3k²) - 6√2 kx - 9 = 0.So, this is a quadratic in x: [1 - 3k²]x² - [6√2 k]x - 9 = 0.Let me denote the coefficients as:A = 1 - 3k²,B = -6√2 k,C = -9.For the quadratic equation Ax² + Bx + C = 0 to have real solutions, the discriminant must be non-negative. However, since we need two distinct intersection points (points A and B), the discriminant must be positive. Also, both solutions must correspond to x ≤ -√3. So, first, discriminant > 0, and second, both roots are ≤ -√3.So, first step: compute discriminant D = B² - 4AC.Compute D:D = (-6√2 k)^2 - 4*(1 - 3k²)*(-9).Compute each term:First term: (-6√2 k)^2 = 36*(2)*k² = 72k².Second term: -4*(1 - 3k²)*(-9) = -4*(-9)*(1 - 3k²) = 36*(1 - 3k²).Therefore, D = 72k² + 36*(1 - 3k²) = 72k² + 36 - 108k² = (72k² - 108k²) + 36 = (-36k²) + 36 = 36(1 - k²).So discriminant D = 36(1 - k²). For real and distinct solutions, we need D > 0. So:36(1 - k²) > 0 ⇒ 1 - k² > 0 ⇒ k² < 1 ⇒ |k| < 1. So initially, k must be between -1 and 1. But we also need both roots to be ≤ -√3.Wait, but hyperbola left branch is x ≤ -√3, so the x-coordinates of the intersection points must satisfy x ≤ -√3. Therefore, we need both roots of the quadratic in x to be ≤ -√3.So, in the quadratic equation Ax² + Bx + C = 0, with A = 1 - 3k², B = -6√2 k, C = -9, the roots x1 and x2 must satisfy x1 ≤ -√3 and x2 ≤ -√3.To find the conditions for both roots to be ≤ -√3, we can use the following criteria for quadratic equations:1. The quadratic must open upwards or downwards depending on the coefficient A. If A > 0, it opens upwards; if A < 0, it opens downward. However, since we need both roots to be ≤ -√3, the direction of the parabola affects the conditions.2. The vertex of the parabola must lie to the left of -√3.3. The quadratic evaluated at x = -√3 must be ≥ 0 if the parabola opens upwards (since the vertex is to the left, and the parabola opens upwards, the function is increasing to the right of the vertex, so if f(-√3) ≥ 0, then all x ≤ -√3 will have f(x) ≥ 0). Wait, but actually, since we have the equation Ax² + Bx + C = 0, and we want both roots to be ≤ -√3, the conditions are:For A > 0:- The quadratic must have two real roots.- The axis of symmetry must be ≤ -√3.- f(-√3) ≥ 0.For A < 0:- The quadratic must have two real roots.- The axis of symmetry must be ≤ -√3.- f(-√3) ≤ 0.But since the quadratic equation here is [1 - 3k²]x² -6√2 kx -9 = 0, the coefficient A is 1 - 3k².So first, let's check the leading coefficient A = 1 - 3k².Case 1: A > 0 ⇒ 1 - 3k² > 0 ⇒ k² < 1/3 ⇒ |k| < 1/√3 ≈ 0.577.Case 2: A < 0 ⇒ k² > 1/3 ⇒ |k| > 1/√3.But we already have from the discriminant that |k| < 1, so combining with Case 2, we have 1/√3 < |k| < 1.Now, depending on the case, the conditions for both roots ≤ -√3 differ.So let's handle each case.First, let's write the quadratic equation as:(1 - 3k²)x² -6√2 kx -9 = 0.Let me denote the quadratic function as f(x) = (1 - 3k²)x² -6√2 kx -9.We need both roots of f(x) = 0 to be ≤ -√3.Let's recall the conditions for both roots to be ≤ some value m (here m = -√3).For a quadratic equation ax² + bx + c = 0:1. The equation has real roots: discriminant D ≥ 0.2. The sum of the roots, -b/a, is ≤ 2m (since the average of the roots is -b/(2a) ≤ m).3. The product of the roots, c/a, is ≥ m² (if a > 0) or ≤ m² (if a < 0). Wait, actually, when a > 0, for both roots ≤ m, then f(m) ≥ 0 because the parabola opens upwards and if both roots are left of m, then f(m) is positive. Similarly, if a < 0, the parabola opens downward, and f(m) ≤ 0.Alternatively, perhaps another way to think is:If the quadratic has two real roots, then both roots are ≤ m if and only if:1. The quadratic has real roots (D ≥ 0).2. The axis of symmetry is ≤ m.3. f(m) has the same sign as the leading coefficient (if a > 0, f(m) ≥ 0; if a < 0, f(m) ≤ 0).So, applying that here, m = -√3.So, first, we have already discriminant D = 36(1 - k²) > 0 ⇒ |k| < 1.Second, the axis of symmetry for the quadratic f(x) is x = -B/(2A) = (6√2 k)/(2(1 - 3k²)) = (3√2 k)/(1 - 3k²). Wait, B is -6√2 k, so -B/(2A) is (6√2 k)/(2*(1 - 3k²)) = (3√2 k)/(1 - 3k²). So the axis of symmetry is x = (3√2 k)/(1 - 3k²). We need this axis of symmetry to be ≤ -√3.So, (3√2 k)/(1 - 3k²) ≤ -√3.Third, f(-√3) should be ≥ 0 if A > 0, and ≤ 0 if A < 0.So let's handle these conditions.First, let's split into cases based on the leading coefficient A.Case 1: A > 0, i.e., 1 - 3k² > 0 ⇒ |k| < 1/√3 ≈ 0.577.In this case, the parabola opens upwards. Therefore, to have both roots ≤ -√3, we need:1. The axis of symmetry ≤ -√3.2. f(-√3) ≥ 0.Case 2: A < 0, i.e., |k| > 1/√3.In this case, the parabola opens downward. Therefore, to have both roots ≤ -√3, we need:1. The axis of symmetry ≤ -√3.2. f(-√3) ≤ 0.So, let's handle each case.Starting with Case 1: A > 0 (|k| < 1/√3)First, compute the axis of symmetry:x = (3√2 k)/(1 - 3k²). We need this ≤ -√3.So:(3√2 k)/(1 - 3k²) ≤ -√3.Multiply both sides by (1 - 3k²). But since A = 1 - 3k² > 0 in this case, multiplying doesn't change the inequality direction. So:3√2 k ≤ -√3 (1 - 3k²)Bring all terms to the left:3√2 k + √3 (1 - 3k²) ≤ 0.Factor out √3:√3 [ (3√2 / √3)k + (1 - 3k²) ] ≤ 0.Simplify (3√2 / √3) = 3*(√6 / 3) = √6. Wait, let's see:√2 / √3 = √(2/3). So 3*(√2 / √3) = 3*√(2/3) = √(9*(2/3)) = √6. Yes.Therefore:√3 [ √6 k + 1 - 3k² ] ≤ 0.Since √3 > 0, we can divide both sides by √3:√6 k + 1 - 3k² ≤ 0.Rearranged:-3k² + √6 k + 1 ≤ 0.Multiply both sides by -1 (inequality reverses):3k² - √6 k - 1 ≥ 0.Solve 3k² - √6 k - 1 ≥ 0.This is a quadratic in k. Let's find its roots.Discriminant D' = ( -√6 )² - 4*3*(-1) = 6 + 12 = 18.So roots are [ √6 ± √18 ] / (2*3) = [ √6 ± 3√2 ] / 6.Simplify √18 = 3√2.So:First root: [√6 + 3√2]/6Second root: [√6 - 3√2]/6Approximate values:√6 ≈ 2.45, √2 ≈ 1.414, so 3√2 ≈ 4.242.First root: (2.45 + 4.242)/6 ≈ 6.692/6 ≈ 1.115Second root: (2.45 - 4.242)/6 ≈ (-1.792)/6 ≈ -0.299.So the quadratic 3k² - √6 k - 1 is ≥ 0 when k ≤ [√6 - 3√2]/6 ≈ -0.299 or k ≥ [√6 + 3√2]/6 ≈ 1.115.But in Case 1, |k| < 1/√3 ≈ 0.577. So the interval where 3k² - √6 k -1 ≥ 0 in this context is k ≤ -0.299. However, since k must also satisfy |k| < 1/√3 ≈0.577, the valid interval here is k ≤ -0.299, but since [√6 - 3√2]/6 is approximately -0.299, so k ≤ [√6 - 3√2]/6.But we need to write exact expressions, not decimal approximations.Note that [√6 - 3√2]/6 can be written as (√6 - 3√2)/6.But let's see if we can simplify it:√6 = √(2*3) = √2√3, 3√2 = 3√2.So:(√2√3 - 3√2)/6 = √2(√3 - 3)/6 = (√3 - 3)/ (6/√2) ) Hmm, maybe not helpful. Alternatively, factor √2:= √2(√3 - 3)/6 = (√3 - 3)/ (6/√2) ) Not sure. Maybe leave as is.So, in Case 1, where |k| < 1/√3, the condition 3k² - √6 k -1 ≥ 0 holds when k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6 ≈ -0.299. So combining with |k| < 1/√3 ≈ 0.577, the valid k in Case 1 is k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6 and |k| < 1/√3. Since (√6 - 3√2)/6 ≈ -0.299, which is greater than -1/√3 ≈ -0.577, so the interval is (√6 - 3√2)/6 ≤ k < 1/√3. Wait, no: original inequality is 3k² - √6 k -1 ≥ 0. So k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6 or k ≥ (√6 + 3√2)/6. But in Case 1, |k| < 1/√3 ≈0.577, and (√6 + 3√2)/6 ≈1.115, which is outside the Case 1 range, so only k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6. Therefore, in Case 1, the solution is k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6.But we need to check if this is within |k| <1/√3. Since (√6 - 3√2)/6 ≈ -0.299, which is greater than -1/√3 ≈ -0.577. So the valid k in Case 1 is -1/√3 < k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6. Wait, but (√6 - 3√2)/6 is approximately -0.299, which is greater than -0.577. So the interval is from -1/√3 up to (√6 - 3√2)/6. But since the inequality in Case 1 is 3k² - √6 k -1 ≥ 0 which gives k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6 or k ≥ (√6 + 3√2)/6. But in Case 1, we have |k| < 1/√3 ≈0.577. So only the k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6 part is valid here. So the k's in Case 1 that satisfy the axis of symmetry condition are k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6. However, since in Case 1, k is between -1/√3 and 1/√3, and (√6 - 3√2)/6 ≈-0.299 is greater than -1/√3 ≈-0.577, so the actual interval is -1/√3 < k ≤ (√6 - 3√2)/6.Additionally, in Case 1, we also need f(-√3) ≥ 0. Let's compute f(-√3):f(-√3) = (1 - 3k²)(-√3)^2 -6√2 k*(-√3) -9.Compute term by term:(-√3)^2 = 3.So:(1 - 3k²)*3 -6√2 k*(-√3) -9.Simplify:3(1 - 3k²) + 6√2*√3 k -9.Simplify each term:3 - 9k² + 6√6 k -9.Combine constants: 3 -9 = -6.So:-6 -9k² +6√6 k.Therefore, f(-√3) = -9k² +6√6 k -6.We need this to be ≥0 (since A >0 in Case1).So:-9k² +6√6 k -6 ≥0.Multiply both sides by -1 (reverse inequality):9k² -6√6 k +6 ≤0.Divide by 3:3k² -2√6 k +2 ≤0.Solve 3k² -2√6 k +2 ≤0.Find discriminant:D'' = (-2√6)^2 -4*3*2 = 24 -24 =0.So discriminant is zero, so quadratic touches the x-axis at k = [2√6]/(2*3) = √6/3 ≈0.816.Therefore, 3k² -2√6 k +2 is a perfect square: (√3 k - √2 )² *3? Wait, let's check.Wait, 3k² -2√6 k +2.Let me factor it:Assume it's (a k - b)^2 = a²k² - 2abk + b².Comparing:a² =3, so a=√3.2ab = 2√6 ⇒ ab=√6. Since a=√3, then b=√6 /√3= √2.Therefore, (√3 k - √2)^2 = 3k² -2√6 k +2. Exactly. So the quadratic is (√3 k - √2)^2 ≤0.But square is always ≥0, so equality holds only when √3 k - √2 =0 ⇒k=√2/√3=√(2/3)=√6/3≈0.816.But the inequality (√3 k - √2)^2 ≤0 only holds when k=√6/3. However, in our Case 1, |k| <1/√3≈0.577, and √6/3≈0.816 is outside this range. Therefore, in Case1, 3k² -2√6 k +2 ≤0 has no solution. Therefore, f(-√3) ≥0 has no solution in Case1. Therefore, there are no solutions in Case1.Therefore, even though we had k ≤ (√6 -3√2)/6 ≈-0.299 in Case1, the condition f(-√3) ≥0 cannot be satisfied in this case. Therefore, Case1 yields no valid k.Now, moving on to Case2: A <0, i.e., |k| >1/√3≈0.577. But we still have |k| <1 from discriminant condition. So, 1/√3 < |k| <1.In this case, the parabola opens downward (since A =1 -3k² <0). Therefore, to have both roots ≤ -√3, we need:1. The axis of symmetry ≤ -√3.2. f(-√3) ≤0.Let's check the axis of symmetry first:x = (3√2 k)/(1 -3k²). But since A <0, denominator 1 -3k² <0. So, x = (3√2 k)/(negative number). So x is negative if k is positive, and positive if k is negative. Wait, let's think.Wait, if |k| >1/√3, then k can be positive or negative. Let's split into k >1/√3 and k < -1/√3.But since we have |k| <1 overall, so 1/√3 <k<1 or -1 <k< -1/√3.First, let's consider k positive: 1/√3 <k<1.Then, x = (3√2 k)/(1 -3k²). Since denominator 1 -3k² <0 (because 3k² >1), and numerator 3√2 k >0, so overall x is negative. Similarly, if k is negative: -1 <k< -1/√3, then numerator 3√2 k is negative, denominator 1 -3k² is negative (since 3k² >1), so x is positive (negative divided by negative). Wait, so:For k >1/√3, axis of symmetry x is negative.For k < -1/√3, axis of symmetry x is positive.But we need the axis of symmetry to be ≤ -√3. So for k >1/√3, since x is negative, it's possible. For k < -1/√3, x is positive, which can't be ≤ -√3. Therefore, in Case2, only k >1/√3 are candidates for axis of symmetry ≤ -√3. The k < -1/√3 will have axis of symmetry positive, which cannot satisfy ≤ -√3.Therefore, in Case2, we only need to consider 1/√3 <k<1.So, for 1/√3 <k <1, compute the axis of symmetry:x = (3√2 k)/(1 -3k²). Since denominator is negative (1 -3k² <0), and numerator positive (k>0), so x is negative. We need x ≤ -√3.Therefore:(3√2 k)/(1 -3k²) ≤ -√3.Multiply both sides by (1 -3k²). But since denominator is negative (as 1 -3k² <0), multiplying both sides reverses inequality direction:3√2 k ≥ -√3 (1 -3k²).Simplify:3√2 k ≥ -√3 +3√3 k².Bring all terms to left:3√2 k +√3 -3√3 k² ≥0.Factor out √3:√3 [ (3√2 /√3 )k +1 -3k² ] ≥0.Simplify (3√2 /√3 ) = 3*(√6 /3 )= √6.So:√3 [ √6 k +1 -3k² ] ≥0.Divide both sides by √3 (positive, so inequality direction remains):√6 k +1 -3k² ≥0.Rearrange:-3k² +√6 k +1 ≥0.Multiply by -1 (reverse inequality):3k² -√6 k -1 ≤0.This is the same quadratic as in Case1: 3k² -√6 k -1 ≤0.From earlier, the roots are k = [√6 ±3√2]/6≈ [2.45±4.24]/6, which gave roots≈1.115 and ≈-0.299.So, the inequality 3k² -√6 k -1 ≤0 holds between the roots: -0.299 ≤k ≤1.115.But in our current case, k is in (1/√3≈0.577,1). Therefore, the overlap is 0.577 <k ≤1.115. But since in this case k <1, the valid interval is 0.577 <k <1.But let's write exact expressions. The roots are (√6 +3√2)/6 and (√6 -3√2)/6. So the inequality holds between (√6 -3√2)/6 ≈-0.299 and (√6 +3√2)/6≈1.115. Since we are in the case k >1/√3≈0.577, the overlap is (1/√3, (√6 +3√2)/6 ). Wait, but (√6 +3√2)/6≈1.115, which is greater than1. However, from discriminant condition, k must be less than1. So the valid interval is (1/√3,1). But we need to check if between 1/√3 and1, 3k² -√6 k -1 ≤0 holds.Wait, let's test k=1. At k=1:3(1)^2 -√6(1) -1=3 -√6 -1≈2 -2.45≈-0.45<0. So at k=1, the inequality holds.At k=1/√3≈0.577:3*(1/3) -√6*(1/√3) -1=1 - (√6/√3) -1= -√2≈-1.414<0. So at k=1/√3, the value is negative.But the quadratic 3k² -√6 k -1 is a parabola opening upwards (coefficient 3>0). So between its roots (approx -0.299 and1.115), the quadratic is ≤0. Therefore, in the interval (1/√3,1), which is within ( -0.299,1.115), the quadratic is ≤0. Therefore, the inequality 3k² -√6 k -1 ≤0 holds for all k in (1/√3,1). Finding the equation of the hyperbola ( C ): - The real axis length is ( 2sqrt{3} ), so ( 2a = 2sqrt{3} ) implies ( a = sqrt{3} ). - The distance to the focus ( c = 2 ). - Using the relationship ( c^2 = a^2 + b^2 ), we have ( 4 = 3 + b^2 ), leading to ( b^2 = 1 ). - Therefore, the equation of the hyperbola is ( frac{x^2}{3} - y^2 = 1 ).2. Finding the range of ( k ) for the line ( l: y = kx + sqrt{2} ) intersecting the left branch of the hyperbola: - Substitute ( y = kx + sqrt{2} ) into the hyperbola equation: [ frac{x^2}{3} - (kx + sqrt{2})^2 = 1 ] - Simplify to get a quadratic equation in ( x ): [ (1 - 3k^2)x^2 - 6sqrt{2}kx - 9 = 0 ] - For the line to intersect the left branch at two points, the quadratic must have two real roots with ( x leq -sqrt{3} ). - The discriminant ( D = 36(1 - k^2) ) must be positive, leading to ( |k| < 1 ). - The axis of symmetry of the quadratic must be ( leq -sqrt{3} ) and the quadratic evaluated at ( x = -sqrt{3} ) must be non-negative or non-positive depending on the leading coefficient. - After analyzing the conditions, the valid range for ( k ) is found to be ( left( frac{sqrt{3}}{3}, 1 right) ). Finding the general term formula for the sequence ({a_n}): - Since ({a_n}) is a geometric sequence, we denote the first term as (a_1) and the common ratio as (r). The general term is (a_n = a_1 cdot r^{n-1}). - The sum of the first (n) terms is (S_n = a_1 frac{1 - r^n}{1 - r}) for (r neq 1). - Using the given condition (a_{n+1} = S_n + 1), we substitute (a_{n+1} = a_1 cdot r^n) and (S_n) into the equation: [ a_1 cdot r^n = a_1 frac{1 - r^n}{1 - r} + 1 ] - Solving for (a_1) and (r) by substituting (n = 1) and (n = 2), we find: - For (n = 1): (a_1 r = a_1 + 1) - For (n = 2): (a_1 r^2 = a_1 (1 + r) + 1) - Solving these equations, we find (a_1 = 1) and (r = 2). Finding the general term formula for the sequence ({a_n}): - We start by noting that a geometric sequence has the form (a_n = a_1 cdot r^{n-1}) and the sum of the first (n) terms is (S_n = a_1 frac{1 - r^n}{1 - r}) for (r neq 1). - Using the given condition (a_{n+1} = S_n + 1), we substitute the geometric sequence formulas: [ a_1 cdot r^n = frac{a_1 (1 - r^n)}{1 - r} + 1 ] - Solving for (a_1) and (r) by considering specific values of (n), we find two possible solutions: (r = 2) with (a_1 = 1) and (r = 0) with (a_1 = -1). However, the non-trivial solution (r = 2) leads to a valid geometric sequence for all (n), while the trivial solution (r = 0) results in a sequence that becomes zero after the first term. - The non-trivial solution gives the general term formula: [ a_n = 2^{n-1} ]2. Finding the sum of the first (2n) terms of the sequence ({b_n} = {(-1)^n (a_n + n)}): - Given (b_n = (-1)^n (a_n + n)), we need to find (T_{2n}). - Substituting (a_n = 2^{n-1}) into (b_n), we get: [ b_n = (-1)^n (2^{n-1} + n) ] - We split the sum (T_{2n}) into two parts: [ T_{2n} = sum_{k=1}^{2n} (-1)^k 2^{k-1} + sum_{k=1}^{2n} (-1)^k k ] - The first sum is a geometric series: [ sum_{k=1}^{2n} (-1)^k 2^{k-1} = frac{1}{2} sum_{k=1}^{2n} (-2)^k = frac{1}{2} left( frac{-2 (1 - (-2)^{2n})}{1 + 2} right) = frac{4^n - 1}{3} ] - The second sum is an alternating series: [ sum_{k=1}^{2n} (-1)^k k = n ] - Combining these results, we get: [ T_{2n} = frac{4^n - 1}{3} + n = frac{4^n + 3n - 1}{3} ] Final Answer(1) The general term formula is (boxed{a_n = 2^{n-1}}).(2) The sum of the first (2n) terms (T_{2n}) is (boxed{dfrac{4^n + 3n - 1}{3}}).]

🔑: Solution:# Part (1): Finding the general term formula for the sequence {a_{n}}Given that a_{n+1}=S_{n}+1 and the sequence is geometric with common ratio q, we analyze the sequence as follows:- For n=1, we have: [ a_{2}=S_{1}+1=a_{1}+1 ] Since a_{2}=a_{1}q, it follows that a_{1}q=a_{1}+1.- For ngeqslant 2, we consider: [ a_{n}=S_{n-1}+1 ] Subtracting this from the given a_{n+1}=S_{n}+1, we find: [ a_{n+1}-a_{n}=a_{n} implies frac{a_{n+1}}{a_{n}}=2 ] Therefore, the common ratio q=2.Given q=2, we can deduce that a_{1}=1, and thus the general term formula for the sequence is:[a_{n}=2^{n-1}]# Part (2): Finding the sum of the first 2n terms of the sequence {b_{n}}Given a_{n}=2^{n-1}, we can express {b_n} as:[b_{n}=(-1)^{n}(2^{n-1}+n)]For even and odd terms, we have:- b_{2n}=2^{2n-1}+2n- b_{2n-1}=-(2^{2n-2}+2n-1)Adding b_{2n} and b_{2n-1} gives:[b_{2n}+b_{2n-1}=4^{n-1}+1]The sum of the first 2n terms, T_{2n}, is then the sum of these pairs:[T_{2n}=(b_{1}+b_{2})+(b_{3}+b_{4})+cdots+(b_{2n-1}+b_{2n})=left(1+4+cdots+4^{n-1}right)+n]Using the formula for the sum of a geometric series, we find:[T_{2n}=frac{4^n-1}{3}+n]Therefore, the sum of the first 2n terms of the sequence {b_{n}} is boxed{frac{4^n-1}{3}+n}.

🔑:If in a square of four cells there are two kings, then they attack each other.## SolutionWe will divide the board into 16 squares 2 times 2.Estimate. First, we note that a superstitious number ( N ) must satisfy ( N = 13 times S(N) ), where ( S(N) ) is the sum of the digits of ( N ). We consider the range of possible values for ( N ). The maximum sum of digits for a ( d )-digit number is ( 9d ), so ( N leq 13 times 9d ). For ( d )-digit numbers, ( N geq 10^{d-1} ). Solving ( 10^{d-1} leq 13 times 9d ), we find that ( d ) can be at most 3. Therefore, ( N ) must be a 1-digit, 2-digit, or 3-digit number.We then check values of ( k ) (where ( N = 13k )) from 1 to 76 (since ( 13 times 76 = 988 )) to find ( k ) such that the sum of the digits of ( 13k ) equals ( k ). Single-digit numbers: - For ( N ) to be a single-digit number, ( N = 13 times N ) implies ( N = 0 ). Thus, 0 is a superstitious number.2. Two-digit numbers: - Let ( N = 10a + b ). The equation becomes ( 10a + b = 13(a + b) ), leading to ( 3a + 12b = 0 ). Since ( a geq 1 ), there are no solutions.3. Three-digit numbers: - Let ( N = 100a + 10b + c ). The equation becomes ( 100a + 10b + c = 13(a + b + c) ), simplifying to ( 29a = b + 4c ). - For ( a = 1 ), solving ( 29 = b + 4c ) yields valid solutions: - ( b = 1, c = 7 ) gives ( N = 117 ) - ( b = 5, c = 6 ) gives ( N = 156 ) - ( b = 9, c = 5 ) gives ( N = 195 )4. Four-digit and higher numbers: - The left side of the equation (derived from the number's digits) is much larger than the right side (sum of digits multiplied by 13), making solutions impossible.Thus, the superstitious numbers are ( 0 ), ( 117 ), ( 156 ), and ( 195 ).[boxed{0}, boxed{117}, boxed{156}, boxed{195}]]

🔑:1. Define the problem: We need to find all numbers that are equal to 13 times the sum of their digits. We call such numbers *superstitious*.2. Consider two-digit numbers: Let the two-digit number be represented as (10a + b), where (a) and (b) are the digits. [ 10a + b = 13(a + b) ] Simplify the equation: [ 10a + b = 13a + 13b ] [ 10a + b - 13a - 13b = 0 ] [ -3a - 12b = 0 ] [ a + 4b = 0 ] Since (a) and (b) are digits (0-9), there are no non-negative integer solutions for (a) and (b) that satisfy this equation. Therefore, there are no two-digit superstitious numbers.3. Consider three-digit numbers: Let the three-digit number be represented as (100a + 10b + c), where (a), (b), and (c) are the digits. [ 100a + 10b + c = 13(a + b + c) ] Simplify the equation: [ 100a + 10b + c = 13a + 13b + 13c ] [ 100a + 10b + c - 13a - 13b - 13c = 0 ] [ 87a - 3b - 12c = 0 ] [ 29a = b + 4c ] Since (a), (b), and (c) are digits (0-9), we need to find values that satisfy this equation. 4. Solve for (a = 1): [ 29 cdot 1 = b + 4c ] [ 29 = b + 4c ] We need to find (b) and (c) such that this equation holds: - If (c = 7), then (b = 29 - 4 cdot 7 = 29 - 28 = 1). This gives the number (100 cdot 1 + 10 cdot 1 + 7 = 117). - If (c = 6), then (b = 29 - 4 cdot 6 = 29 - 24 = 5). This gives the number (100 cdot 1 + 10 cdot 5 + 6 = 156). - If (c = 5), then (b = 29 - 4 cdot 5 = 29 - 20 = 9). This gives the number (100 cdot 1 + 10 cdot 9 + 5 = 195).5. Verify the solutions: - For (117): [ 1 + 1 + 7 = 9 quad text{and} quad 13 cdot 9 = 117 ] - For (156): [ 1 + 5 + 6 = 12 quad text{and} quad 13 cdot 12 = 156 ] - For (195): [ 1 + 9 + 5 = 15 quad text{and} quad 13 cdot 15 = 195 ]Thus, the three-digit superstitious numbers are (117), (156), and (195).The final answer is (boxed{117, 156, 195}).

( 2m = p )2. ( 2n = q )3. ( m + 1 = 2p + 2 )4. ( n + 2 = 2q + 1 )Solving these equations step-by-step:- From equation 1: ( p = 2m )- From equation 2: ( q = 2n )- Substituting ( p = 2m ) into equation 3: ( m + 1 = 4m + 2 ) leads to ( m = -frac{1}{3} )- Substituting ( q = 2n ) into equation 4: ( n + 2 = 4n + 1 ) leads to ( n = frac{1}{3} )Substituting ( m ) and ( n ) back, we get ( p = -frac{2}{3} ) and ( q = frac{2}{3} ).Substituting these values back into the polynomial expressions, we get:[ P(x) = -frac{2}{3}x^3 + frac{2}{3}x^2 + frac{2}{3}x + frac{7}{3} ]Verification by polynomial division confirms the remainders are correct. Thus, the polynomial is:[boxed{-dfrac{2}{3}x^3 + dfrac{2}{3}x^2 + dfrac{2}{3}x + dfrac{7}{3}}]]

🔑:SOLUTION. When dividing (with remainder) a polynomial of the third degree by a polynomial of the second degree, the quotient is equal to a polynomial of the first degree. The coefficient of the first power in it is equal to the ratio of the coefficients of the highest powers of the dividend and divisor, while the absolute term of the quotient is unknown, which we denote as e, resp. g for the two given divisions. For the sought polynomial, the following must holdbegin{aligned}& a x^{3}+b x^{2}+c x+d=left(2 x^{2}+1right)left(frac{a}{2} x+eright)+x+2=a x^{3}+2 e x^{2}+left(frac{a}{2}+1right) x+e+2 & a x^{3}+b x^{2}+c x+d=left(x^{2}+2right)(a x+g)+2 x+1=a x^{3}+g x^{2}+(2 a+2) x+2 g+1end{aligned}By comparing the coefficients of the right sides of the previous two equalities, we getbegin{aligned}2 e & =g, frac{a}{2}+1 & =2 a+2, e+2 & =2 g+1 .end{aligned}The solution to this system is a=-frac{2}{3}, g=frac{2}{3}, e=frac{1}{3}. Therefore, the problem is satisfied by the unique polynomiala x^{3}+b x^{2}+c x+d=-frac{2}{3} x^{3}+frac{2}{3} x^{2}+frac{2}{3} x+frac{7}{3} .GUIDING PROBLEMS:N1. Express this fact in the form of an equality without using fractions. left[2 x^{3}+x^{2}-3 x+5=left(x^{2}-xright) timesright. times(2 x+3)+5]N2. Find all trinomials p(x)=a x^{2}+b x+c that give a remainder of 2 when divided by the binomial x+1 and a remainder of 1 when divided by the binomial x+2, with p(1)=61. [61-C-I-1]N4. Find all pairs p, q of real numbers such that the polynomial x^{2}+p x+q is a divisor of the polynomial x^{4}+p x^{2}+q cdot [56-mathrm{B}-mathrm{I}-5]

🔑:6. 5 sqrt{2} Detailed Explanation: As shown in the figure, connect A B_{1}, and unfold triangle C_{1} B_{1} C along B_{1} C onto the plane A B_{1} C. Then, the minimum value of A P+P C_{1} is the length of segment A C_{1}. In triangle A C C_{1}, A C=6, C C_{1}=sqrt{2}, angle A C C_{1}=angle A C B_{1} + angle B_{1} C C_{1}=135^{circ}. By the cosine rule, we get A C_{1}=sqrt{6^{2}+(sqrt{2})^{2}-2 times 6 sqrt{2} cos 135^{circ}}=5 sqrt{2}.
